    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer14505752760231433223"><i>Alternate cover edition of ISBN10: 0140422536; ISBN13: 9780140422535</i><br/><br/>Written in the middle of his career, Idylls of the King is Tennyson's longest and most ambitious work. Reflecting his lifelong interest in Arthurian themes, his primary sources were Malory's Morte d'Arthur and the Welsh Mabinogion. For him, the Idylls embodied the universal and unending war between sense a</span>
  <span id="freeText14505752760231433223" style="display:none"><i>Alternate cover edition of ISBN10: 0140422536; ISBN13: 9780140422535</i><br /><br />Written in the middle of his career, Idylls of the King is Tennyson's longest and most ambitious work. Reflecting his lifelong interest in Arthurian themes, his primary sources were Malory's Morte d'Arthur and the Welsh Mabinogion. For him, the Idylls embodied the universal and unending war between sense and soul, and Arthur the highest ideals of manhood and kingship; an attitude totally compatible with the moral outlook of his age. Poetically, Tennyson was heir to the Romantics, and Keats's influence in particular can be seen clearly in much of his work. Yet Tennyson's style is undoubtedly his own and he achieved a delicacy of phrase and subtlety of metrical effect that are unmatched. This edition, based on the text authorized by Tennyson himself, contains full critical apparatus.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14505752760231433223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_133155690" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133155690" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1309716-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529984339p2/1309716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133155690?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1309716-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781175941466" href="/book/show/9262023-idylls-of-the-king">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133155690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer411008077851201935">I have a beautiful, old edition of this book. I wish I could show you.<br/><br/>On the book marker, in old-fashioned cursive, it says,<br/><i>Merry Christmas<br/>To Lottie<br/>from<br/>Dora</i><br/><br/><b>Update:</b><br/>This is a truly beautiful work. Enchanting. Mesmerizing, really.<br/>There is just <i>one little thing</i> though...<br/>I'd heard rumblings of this book being misogynistic. Loving Tennyson as I do, I refused to believe it. Basically, I read the book like this:<br/>"Well, that's not <i>necessarily</i> sexist...Okay, it is. But, surely he didn't <i>intend</i>...Okay, he d</span>
  <span id="freeText411008077851201935" style="display:none">I have a beautiful, old edition of this book. I wish I could show you.<br /><br />On the book marker, in old-fashioned cursive, it says,<br /><i>Merry Christmas<br />To Lottie<br />from<br />Dora</i><br /><br /><b>Update:</b><br />This is a truly beautiful work. Enchanting. Mesmerizing, really.<br />There is just <i>one little thing</i> though...<br />I'd heard rumblings of this book being misogynistic. Loving Tennyson as I do, I refused to believe it. Basically, I read the book like this:<br />"Well, that's not <i>necessarily</i> sexist...Okay, it is. But, surely he didn't <i>intend</i>...Okay, he did. But, that doesn't make it some kind of misogynist manifesto!...Bloody Hell."<br /><br />He's not just saying that women had a certain role or that certain women had a negative influence. He's <i>clearly</i> saying that women only hinder a man's more noble pursuits. Though there are good women in the book, they have little influence over events. Though there are bad men, they are likewise secondary or portrayed as deeply conflicted. Seems to me, when your only choices are, "naive virgin," "adulterous bitch," "frigid bitch," and "bitch," you're conflicted! But Tennyson's women, excepting Elaine and Guinevere, are one dimensional. It would seem Dora's message from the great beyond is, "Merry Christmas. Shut your whore face."<br /><br />At this point, you're probably wondering why I gave this book five stars if I hated it...<br /><br />I loved it.<br /><br />You see, there's just something <i>about</i> it, an otherworldly beauty. Not just beauty but undeniable <i>truth</i>. I love the tragic Elaine and the wantonly destructive Lancelot. I love Guinevere's incapacity for quiet contentment. I love how the holy quest for the grail was soured by by pride and greed. I love Enid's sweetness and Lynette's hilarious bitchiness. I love Arthur's high ideals and his bitter disillusionment. Most of all, I love the glimpse into Tennyson's own tortured psyche. Because, when you really look at it, this isn't a morality tale at all. It's loss of innocence. It's human nature. It's, by God, we really tried.<br /><br /><i>Then Arthur rose and Lancelot followed him,<br />And while they stood without the doors, the King<br />Turned to him saying, `Is it then so well?<br />Or mine the blame that oft I seem as he<br />Of whom was written, "A sound is in his ears"?<br />The foot that loiters, bidden go,--the glance<br />That only seems half-loyal to command,--<br />A manner somewhat fallen from reverence--<br />Or have I dreamed the bearing of our knights<br />Tells of a manhood ever less and lower?<br />Or whence the fear lest this my realm, upreared,<br />By noble deeds at one with noble vows,<br />From flat confusion and brute violences,<br />Reel back into the beast, and be no more?'<br /><br />He spoke, and taking all his younger knights,<br />Down the slope city rode, and sharply turned<br />North by the gate. In her high bower the Queen,<br />Working a tapestry, lifted up her head,<br />Watched her lord pass, and knew not that she sighed.</i><br /><br />I am a whore, dammit! A whore for Tennyson!<br /><br />"You don't have to shout."<br /><br />Sorry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="411008077851201935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The saddest lines for me are (spoken by Arthur to Guinevere visiting her in the nunnery before the final battle with Modred):<br/><br/>"Thou hast not made my life so sweet to me,<br/>That I the King should greatly care to live;<br/>For thou hast spoilt the purpose of my life."<br/><br/>The agony in those lines</span>
  <span id="freeText13556079239254363756" style="display:none">I have read my softcover copy so many times it is falling apart. I really need to get a nice, illustrated, hard cover. I read this book several times a year. And sob hysterically at the end so that I can hardly finish. The saddest lines for me are (spoken by Arthur to Guinevere visiting her in the nunnery before the final battle with Modred):<br /><br />"Thou hast not made my life so sweet to me,<br />That I the King should greatly care to live;<br />For thou hast spoilt the purpose of my life."<br /><br />The agony in those lines! And her lying there, with her head in the sand at his feet, too ashamaed to even look at him, and him heartbroken, and yet, still he must be king. Having just come from battling his best friend and best knight and knowing he rides off to his death. It's awful. The poetry is not only beautiful, but in such short bursts, Tennyson is able to capture such powerful emotions. </span>

<div id="review_2505002847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2505002847" />
    <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur"><img alt="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602184090p2/16951301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2505002847?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur">Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=books-i-read-in-2018">books-i-read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2505002847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11187024614833818011"><i>Idylls of the King</i> is something I've wanted to read since I was a teenager--the title, the subject matter, the format all mixed together in my mind to suggest a work of astonishing grace and beauty. I might also add that I had a very naïve idea of what literature was capable of at that time--whatever the actual effect that literature might have on the reader, I was under the confused impression that reading classics like this would somehow augment me as a person, in the same way as someone who h</span>
  <span id="freeText11187024614833818011" style="display:none"><i>Idylls of the King</i> is something I've wanted to read since I was a teenager--the title, the subject matter, the format all mixed together in my mind to suggest a work of astonishing grace and beauty. I might also add that I had a very naïve idea of what literature was capable of at that time--whatever the actual effect that literature might have on the reader, I was under the confused impression that reading classics like this would somehow augment me as a person, in the same way as someone who happens to have a lot of money might think their personal value was increased as well.<br /><br />Suffice it to say that I realize now the only thing that makes someone a better person is what they <i>do</i> rather than what they <i>know</i>; great literature might assist in the decision making process by enlarging one's capacity for empathy, but as trophies on a shelf they don't mean much. Taking that into consideration, reading <i>Idylls of the King</i> back when I was a teenager (even assuming I would have finished it) would have been mostly useless, I think. It <i>is</i> a work of grace and beauty, but I would never have been able to see that. Probably all I would have been able to absorb was the fact that there was a lot less derring-do than I would have expected, and as a result, let the words pass in front of my eyes till I was done.<br /><br />Without some help, that might also have been my experience all these decades later. Thankfully, I had Harold Littledale's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3227030.Essays_on_Lord_Tennyson_s_Idylls_of_the_King" title="Essays on Lord Tennyson's Idylls of the King by Harold Littledale" rel="nofollow noopener">Essays on Lord Tennyson's Idylls of the King</a>, a (fre)e-book I found on Google Books. I doubt that was the best resource, but it was enough, and the price was right. Reading one of Littledale's essays just prior to reading one of the idylls made a tremendous difference--since I was familiar with the story, spoilers weren't an issue, and with Littledale's short summary and some explanatory notes in hand, I was able to concentrate on the poetry rather than have to split my attention and try to divine the action at the same time. <br /><br />Another GR poster summed up very succinctly my ideas about the <i>Idylls</i> before I'd started reading: "The title of this work...led me to believe that is would be a fairly cheerful work, but boy was I wrong." I took <i>Idyll</i> to mean <i>idyllic</i>, which made me think of innocence and pleasant summer days and happily ever afters. Instead, it struck me as a deeply melancholy book, with a spiritual subtext that added to the pervading sense of impending doom. This wasn't apparent in the beginning--the first five books held to what I initially expected, but with <i>Balin and Balan</i>, I thought there was a sharp turn in direction. <br /><br />Or, at least, a sharp turn if you aren't expecting it. But in looking back, it's part of a unified whole. Arthur comes to power with the goal of creating a new, chivalrous existence for his kingdom and his people, a return to a kind of golden age where both the clash of arms and spiritual reverence coexist. But, although Arthur himself may be capable of such purity, almost everyone he gathers around him is not, and from the very beginning, the illicit love of Lancelot and Guinevere causes a cancer that rots his kingdom away from the inside out. <br /><br />Those who are familiar with the story surrounding King Arthur will notice some changes that Tennyson adopted--probably the most obvious is the absence of Morgan La Fay, and that Mordred is only Arthur's nephew here, rather than the child of an incestuous union. At first, I thought that these changes would neuter the story, but in the end I decided they did not. Other characters take on different shades of personality--and mostly here I'm thinking of Sir Gawain, who, under Tennyson, is opportunistic and shallow. And the search for the Holy Grail is less an adventure in itself as it is an indication that Arthur failed to bring about the spiritual rebirth that he wanted. But I think these changes work well, considering that Tennyson was telling a different story than Mallory. <br /><br />Altogether I found it absorbing and moving. Five stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="11187024614833818011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1560490436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1560490436" />
    <a title="Abigail Hartman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2272332-abigail-hartman"><img alt="Abigail Hartman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530881804p2/2272332.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1560490436?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail Hartman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail Hartman" href="/user/show/2272332-abigail-hartman">Abigail Hartman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2272332-abigail-hartman?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1560490436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17577753297160421918">Tennyson's poetry is some of the most beautiful I've encountered (admittedly, not saying much, because my acquaintance with poetry is slight): his turns of phrase and the pictures he paints are wonderfully evocative, and there's an eerie mysticism in stories like "The Holy Grail." Even the fatalism -- as the idylls begin in spring and descend into a thoroughly gloomy autumn -- draws you in. Of course, since the unifying theme is the affair between Lancelot and Guinevere and the chaos it brings, </span>
  <span id="freeText17577753297160421918" style="display:none">Tennyson's poetry is some of the most beautiful I've encountered (admittedly, not saying much, because my acquaintance with poetry is slight): his turns of phrase and the pictures he paints are wonderfully evocative, and there's an eerie mysticism in stories like "The Holy Grail." Even the fatalism -- as the idylls begin in spring and descend into a thoroughly gloomy autumn -- draws you in. Of course, since the unifying theme is the affair between Lancelot and Guinevere and the chaos it brings, and since except for the first two stories the idylls are dark as all get out, it's also a depressing read. Also, as in many iterations of the Arthurian legend, there are the obvious spiritual parallels drawn between Arthur and Christ that always strike me as singularly suspect.<br /><br /><i>"But the other swiftly strode from ridge to ridge,<br />Clothed with his breath, and looking, as he walk'd,<br />Larger than human on the frozen hills."</i> - The Passing of Arthur, 349-351<br /><br /><i>"Then from dawn it seem'd there came, but faint<br />As from beyond the limit of the world,<br />Like the last echo born of a great cry,<br />Sounds, as if some fair city were one voice<br />Around a king returning from his wars."</i> - The Passing of Arthur, 457-461</span>
  <a data-text-id="17577753297160421918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15052551186741131860">This book was every bit as beautiful as I could imagine. I had previously loved and read The Lady of Shalott. And you might fall in love with the characters of this legend all over again.<br/><br/>Lancelot - "...a dying fire of madness in his eyes"<br/><br/>Percivale - "Had heaven appear'd so blue, nor earth so green, For all my blood danced in me, and I knew That I s</span>
  <span id="freeText15052551186741131860" style="display:none">This book was every bit as beautiful as I could imagine. I had previously loved and read The Lady of Shalott. Idylls, however, is a testament to his love and knowledge of Arthurian legend. <br /><br />You'll likely walk away from this book with lots of favorite passages. And you might fall in love with the characters of this legend all over again.<br /><br />Lancelot - "...a dying fire of madness in his eyes"<br /><br />Percivale - "Had heaven appear'd so blue, nor earth so green, For all my blood danced in me, and I knew That I should light up on the Holy Grail."<br /><br />Arthur - "...my Lancelot, thou in whom I have most joy and most affiance, for I know What thou hast been in battle by my side... "<br /><br />I love reading Malory's M d'A, but Tennyson's poetry really wraps heartand soul around his interpretation of Malory. <br /><br />These poems were also another living dedication to Tennyson's lost friend Arthur Hallam.<br /><br />Fans of the Arthur legend shouldn't put off reading Idylls any longer!</span>

<div id="review_162943380" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162943380" />
    <a title="David M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1589870-david-m"><img alt="David M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436127740p2/1589870.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/162943380?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="David M." href="/user/show/1589870-david-m">David M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer162943380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1094957618531798215">There are certain books, or authors, that don't hold up to modern political correctness. Mark Twain is one of them; <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> is constantly under threat to be banned from American schools. Robert E. Howard's protagonists routinely face villains who embody the worst of early twentieth century stereotypes. But Tennyson, in <i>Idylls of the King</i> comes under fire for his female characters in his series of epic poems concerning King Arthur and his valorous knights. What is not generally kept in m</span>
  <span id="freeText1094957618531798215" style="display:none">There are certain books, or authors, that don't hold up to modern political correctness. Mark Twain is one of them; <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> is constantly under threat to be banned from American schools. Robert E. Howard's protagonists routinely face villains who embody the worst of early twentieth century stereotypes. But Tennyson, in <i>Idylls of the King</i> comes under fire for his female characters in his series of epic poems concerning King Arthur and his valorous knights. What is not generally kept in mind, is that his source is a 15th century nobleman who was serving time in prison for many crimes, one of which was rape. Tennyson might not have had to rub it in, but that's the time where Arthur comes from, where women had two roles: Mary or Eve. If a woman wasn't a nun, then she was sinful. <br /><p><br />Another facet of this story that seems to be overlooked is that Arthur is not a likable character. When Arthur establishes the court of Camelot, he brings to himself men he believes shares his vision of women adoration, idyllic sporting, and the occasional quest. Some knights do hold to this life, but others, like Tristram and Guinevere, are human beings; it's unreasonable for a saint to hold all to his own morals. Arthur's reproach of Guinevere shows that he married her to win a crown, not to love her like the flesh and blood woman that she was. It's only when he's dying does he wonder that it might not be possible for all to live like him.<br /></p><p>Despite personal views on whether or not Tennyson hated women like no other person, this is an exceptional take on the Arthurian legend, and the Penguin addition contains interesting notes compiled from not just Tennyson, but the author's son as well. Highly reccomended!</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="1094957618531798215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_477352894" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond"><img alt="Bryn Hammond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523356367p2/7224461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryn Hammond" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond">Bryn Hammond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=poets-playwrights">poets-playwrights</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer477352894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6084689434592684817">As usual, I thought right up there the short story of Balin, who is to blame for his own tragedy ('My violences, my violences!'). <br/><br/>Darker than I had expected and gutsier. I think I decided to read this at last after I saw a book on Tennyson's battle poetry. How he wrote 54 battle poems and had a genuine feel for the 'heroic ethos' of ancient fiction to which he was devoted. Fair enough, I thought. Tried a couple of short ones: his Boadicea is as bloody as she came, and I throbbed to 'The Revenge:</span>
  <span id="freeText6084689434592684817" style="display:none">As usual, I thought right up there the short story of Balin, who is to blame for his own tragedy ('My violences, my violences!'). <br /><br />Darker than I had expected and gutsier. I think I decided to read this at last after I saw a book on Tennyson's battle poetry. How he wrote 54 battle poems and had a genuine feel for the 'heroic ethos' of ancient fiction to which he was devoted. Fair enough, I thought. Tried a couple of short ones: his Boadicea is as bloody as she came, and I throbbed to 'The Revenge: a Ballad of the Fleet'. I even felt the tribute in his Ode on Wellington. <br /><br />I think he's a gorgeous poet, on the whole, although I'd make cuts. The guy can write. <br /><br />Arthur is his hero, and not Lancelot. This isn't the courtly love version, but the version where a self-indulgent love corrupts a heroic kingdom. Arthur's certainly a fighting king against pagans. Give him a pagan, he can let loose without qualms and soar with the sword. <br /><br />The comedy can be faux-medieval -- I mean you think of those silly films in tights, but perhaps comedy wasn't his forte. I expect tragedy is, and melancholy.<br /><br />Again, I'll have Balin, ten pages of him, gut-wrenchingly tragic and very darkly done. But I'd say that about Malory's Balin. Which proves to me Tennyson was awake to the old authentic stuff, though he's often condemned for Victorian. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6084689434592684817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_206497054" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206497054" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2623195-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507347653p2/2623195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206497054?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/2623195-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2623195-susan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206497054" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7668039843872226289">Just finished this one for my Victorian Literature seminar. I will admit that the prospect of reading a 300+ page long poem was daunting, but well, well worth it. I have always admired Tennyson's work. This one is a bit different though. The language is not as resonant, but the imagery is spectacularly beautiful. Also, lots of lovely moments of universal truth within the story. They pop out of nowhere sometimes. The characters have a liquid, uncertain quality, bringing a whole lot of ambiguity t</span>
  <span id="freeText7668039843872226289" style="display:none">Just finished this one for my Victorian Literature seminar. I will admit that the prospect of reading a 300+ page long poem was daunting, but well, well worth it. I have always admired Tennyson's work. This one is a bit different though. The language is not as resonant, but the imagery is spectacularly beautiful. Also, lots of lovely moments of universal truth within the story. They pop out of nowhere sometimes. The characters have a liquid, uncertain quality, bringing a whole lot of ambiguity to this story that has been told so many times. The reader is asked over and over to suspend disbelief, not in order to understand, but to become immersed in the imaginary and the inevitable loss of boundaries. Over and over again we are fooled into believing the quickly dissipating fantasy of Tennyson's imagery. Why does Tennyson do this? I feel he did this in order to highlight the vain search for the truth of who we are and how others see us. We need to allow the mist of our emotions to yield and dissipate a little into order to see the truth of the reality of our lives. Beautifully composed, showing King Arthur in an entirely new light. 5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="7668039843872226289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_277020381" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/277020381" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6316460-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567547640p2/6316460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/277020381?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/6316460-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316460-becky?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316460-becky?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316460-becky?shelf=librivox-gutenberg-for-free">librivox-gutenberg-for-free</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316460-becky?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316460-becky?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277020381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16878461344009072580">Idylls of the King is a poetical reworking of the classic tales by Tennyson. It is absolutely breathtaking in its prose, lyrical, but coherent. Flowery without being polluted. It certainly helps to be familiar with the lesser known tales of Arthur's court (such as Balin and Balon) otherwise the reader may have a more difficult time understanding the work.<br/><br/>It really pulls at your heartstrings. You really come to hate all the women of the court, Guinevere most of all. How something written so long </span>
  <span id="freeText16878461344009072580" style="display:none">Idylls of the King is a poetical reworking of the classic tales by Tennyson. It is absolutely breathtaking in its prose, lyrical, but coherent. Flowery without being polluted. It certainly helps to be familiar with the lesser known tales of Arthur's court (such as Balin and Balon) otherwise the reader may have a more difficult time understanding the work.<br /><br />It really pulls at your heartstrings. You really come to hate all the women of the court, Guinevere most of all. How something written so long ago, about a tale even older, can still put so strongly at your emotions is truly indicative of how marvelous and passionate this work is.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16878461344009072580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_78164421" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78164421" />
    <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin"><img alt="Mike (the Paladin)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528431476p2/2187043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78164421?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike (the Paladin)" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin">Mike (the Paladin)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78164421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14790886793135177759">I have more read "in" this than read it "through" at once. I found I loved Tennyson at a time when I had, quite a bit of time on my hands. Tennyson has a voice unlike any you'll find in contemporary writing. In this and other verse he conveys action and emotion. He seems obsessed with misunderstanding, rumor and false assumptions. <br/><br/>Excellently expressed. Enjoy. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1540891531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1540891531" />
    <a title="Rima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5536925-rima"><img alt="Rima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601905257p2/5536925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1540891531?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rima" href="/user/show/5536925-rima">Rima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1540891531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14901445442473321439">Crying. Crying again? How many times have I read the death of King Arthur in several retellings and yet his final moments still cause my heart to cry out in despair? Guinevere and Lancelot's exposed affair, the fall of the Round Table, Mordred's betrayal...it all comes crumbling down.<br/><br/>Loved the way Tennyson evolved the legend but at the same time kept the 12th century atmosphere intact. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_331790363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331790363" />
    <a title="Clif Hostetler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/793473-clif-hostetler"><img alt="Clif Hostetler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593302055p2/793473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331790363?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clif Hostetler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clif Hostetler" href="/user/show/793473-clif-hostetler">Clif Hostetler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/793473-clif-hostetler?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331790363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7236723004735661933"><i>Idylls of the King</i> by Alfred Lord Tennyson was etched into my memory as a famouse classic from an early age thanks to the card game, "Authors." So now, sixty years later, I finally got around to seeing what the story was all about. The final nudge to read it came from a book group so I had the pleasure of discussing the book with others.<br/><br/>So what did I learn? It's the story of King Arthur in blank verse and iambic pentameter, about a hundred pages worth. It's divided into twelve different stories </span>
  <span id="freeText7236723004735661933" style="display:none"><i>Idylls of the King</i> by Alfred Lord Tennyson was etched into my memory as a famouse classic from an early age thanks to the card game, "Authors." So now, sixty years later, I finally got around to seeing what the story was all about. The final nudge to read it came from a book group so I had the pleasure of discussing the book with others.<br /><br />So what did I learn? It's the story of King Arthur in blank verse and iambic pentameter, about a hundred pages worth. It's divided into twelve different stories that are sparingly related to each other. I anticipated encountering the story about Arthur taking the sword out of the stone. But that story wasn't in the book; I guess that story was written another author. <br /><br />The following are some unanswered questions. Is the Lady of the Lake a spirit, ghost or what? What is Merlin, a practitioner of magic, doing in a story that takes place in the Christian era? How could the story be so passionless, and at the same time have an adulterous affair between Lancelot and Guinevere?<br /><br />It seemed to me that Tennyson was taking himself a bit too seriously. He was sort of saying, "Look at me. I'm writing this magnificent poem about the beginnings of English civilization. He dedicates it to Queen Victoria in memory of the deceased Prince Albert. Can't get anymore important that that!<br /><br />The book is a chore to read, and I don't have the patience to truly appreciate it. I think it's a book that needs to read twice, once to read the story and a second time to appreciate the skillful word smithing and poem construction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7236723004735661933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11683871826461889149" style="display:none">I started out kinda dreading this novel because of a) it's thickness and b) its confusingly written self, but as I read it and (cough used some helpful "guides") I have grown to long to read it. <div id="review_551273061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/551273061" />
    <a title="Roland Allnach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10453388-roland-allnach"><img alt="Roland Allnach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1355263574p2/10453388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/551273061?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roland Allnach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roland Allnach" href="/user/show/10453388-roland-allnach">Roland Allnach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140422535" href="/book/show/15720029-idylls-of-the-king">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer551273061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6894797498255760315">A venture of epic poetry, this is Alfred Lord Tennyson's take on the Arthurian legends. An incredible read in its own right, but, when taken in relation to Malory, Tennyson's work highlights the passion, tragedy, and Romanticism that sometimes was left at a simmer in Malory's work. Tennyson's work is more 'fantastic' than Malory's, and his descriptions of Camelot are as awe inspiring for the reader as they are for the characters he follows. For those familiar with Malory, Tennyson does not paral</span>
  <span id="freeText6894797498255760315" style="display:none">A venture of epic poetry, this is Alfred Lord Tennyson's take on the Arthurian legends. An incredible read in its own right, but, when taken in relation to Malory, Tennyson's work highlights the passion, tragedy, and Romanticism that sometimes was left at a simmer in Malory's work. Tennyson's work is more 'fantastic' than Malory's, and his descriptions of Camelot are as awe inspiring for the reader as they are for the characters he follows. For those familiar with Malory, Tennyson does not parallel the tales Malory depicts but rather has his own interpretations to suit his goal in describing the tragic emotional arc of the fall of Camelot. Though I would not consider myself an expert in poetry, I have read quite a few epic pieces, and this is a monumental work with a humanistic focus, anchoring its place among the timeless classics, such as Homer's 'Iliad'. Whether you are a fan of Malory, or Arthurian legend in general, this should be part of your collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6894797498255760315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164233271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17541969081088544797">This particular book is not an edition of Tennyson's work, Idylls of the King, as a whole. It's an abridged version, essentially, with selections from Tennyson's poetry contextualised by brief prose. The reason I have this edition is, of course, the illustrations included, those done by Gustave Doré. Many of them are really spectacular, capturing perfectly the mood of the pieces and scenes.<br/><br/>Tennyson's poetry is, of course, powerful and problematic, but I'll review that in itself another time.</span>

          </span>

He was alright and slightly less idiotic in this though. <br /><br />Elaine is strangely one of Tennyson's more spine-possessing female characters...or the only one. He was able to capture her spirit and yet still her naïveté at the same time. I'm quite sure it's downhill from here with the misogynist rhetoric but we'll see.<br /><br />The writing in this one is extremely reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/724371.The_Lady_of_Shalott" title="The Lady of Shalott by Alfred Tennyson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lady of Shalott</a>, lovely flow!<br /><br /><b>Enid</b><br />Take a shot every time Enid is "meek" and "obedient" because you would rather have alcohol poisoning than read this mess masquerading as a poem.<br /><br /><b>Gareth and Lynette</b><br /> <i>I fly no more: I allow thee for an hour.<br />Lion and stoat have isled together, knave,<br />In time of flood. Nay, furthermore, methinks<br />Some ruth is mine for thee. Back wilt thou, fool?</i><br /><br />Lynette reminds me of Shakespeare's Beatrice (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12957.Much_Ado_About_Nothing" title="Much Ado About Nothing by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">Much Ado About Nothing</a>), I'm surprised Tennyson wrote such a sharp female. Much prefer this version over Malory's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/672875.Le_Morte_d_Arthur_King_Arthur_and_the_Legends_of_the_Round_Table" title="Le Morte d'Arthur King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table by Thomas Malory" rel="nofollow noopener">Le Morte d'Arthur: King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table</a> interpretation of this tale.<br /><br /><b>Merlin and Vivien</b><br />Enjoyed this one significantly less than the others so far but I still couldn't stop reading. This poem attacks you with the honesty of double standards for men and women in Victorian society and the question of which ideals each gender values over all else. I can see how the way in which it is presented can antagonize readers but it depends on how you choose to interpret Tennyson's views. I choose to give him the benefit of the doubt for now.<br /><br /><b>Balin and Balan</b><br /><i>Woods have tongues,<br />As walls have ears: but thou shalt go with me,<br />And we will speak at first exceeding low.<br />Meet is it the good King be not deceived.</i><br /><br />Vivien seems to have forgotten the old adage; snitches get stitches. Also, I know its sad and all but the greatest tragedy in this poem is naming twins Balin and Balan. Reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C_S__Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">C.S. Lewis</a>' Cor and Corin.<br /><br /><b>Pelleas and Ettarre</b><br />I see Geraint is still the saintly, upright knight of the realm despite having an affair with the "bitter", "mock[ing]" and "hate[ful]" Ettarre on whom all the blame lies. And that's on misogyny :)<br /><br /><b>The Coming of Arthur</b><br /><i>Then Arthur charged his warrior whom he loved<br />And honoured most, Sir Lancelot, to ride forth<br />And bring the Queen</i><br /><br />And that's when the trouble started. Very convenient that Bellicent just happened to be strolling through Cameliard right when Leodogran was doubting Arthur's ancestry. Still a very strong opening poem. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6826241800404566948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_409062523" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409062523" />
    <a title="Daniel Lomax" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12599895-daniel-lomax"><img alt="Daniel Lomax" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371068440p2/12599895.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409062523?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Lomax" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Lomax" href="/user/show/12599895-daniel-lomax">Daniel Lomax</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486437958" href="/book/show/393589.Idylls_of_the_King">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409062523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12815382908624746564"><br/>Tennyson combines Shakespeare, Chaucer and Mallory, then drains the blood from the chimera. He manages to make wealth, fame, war and love boring, which I suppose is an achievement of sorts.<br/><br/>Is iambic pentameter really interesting enough to sustain 260 pages? Does this text even benefit from being in verse? It just makes it more of a chore. Chop out the line breaks, I say, and patch together a piece of prose. Truncate the torture. I don't like blank verse generally: the last syllable of every line</span>
  <span id="freeText12815382908624746564" style="display:none"><br />Tennyson combines Shakespeare, Chaucer and Mallory, then drains the blood from the chimera. He manages to make wealth, fame, war and love boring, which I suppose is an achievement of sorts.<br /><br />Is iambic pentameter really interesting enough to sustain 260 pages? Does this text even benefit from being in verse? It just makes it more of a chore. Chop out the line breaks, I say, and patch together a piece of prose. Truncate the torture. I don't like blank verse generally: the last syllable of every line is stressed, then left there hanging, and when the next line doesn't rhyme it feels like an anti-climax every time. Tennyson has to contort the grammar to force it into pentameter: on p132 "Sir Lancelot went ambassador, at first". He did what? Is that like going ape or going postal? On p153 "Full simple was her answer" and on p260 we get "not fail" for "never once failing". This isn't poetic reconstruction, it's bad writing.<br /><br />Because of my style-fascism I've so far overlooked a far bigger issue with the content. All - and I mean all - of the female characters are one-dimensional and detestable, an insipid ballet of Bella Swans. They're self-loathing and it's clear the author loathes them too. At worst they exist to hinder man, and at best they exist to admire him. Tennyson's verse is a veritable manifesto of misogyny.<br /><br />There are writings by George Orwell which lead me to believe Tennyson's short-form poetry is very good, so perhaps I won't give up on him yet...but this book is a bore. Off with its head.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12815382908624746564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_124082561" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124082561" />
    <a title="Amalie " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4164661-amalie"><img alt="Amalie " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600148509p2/4164661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124082561?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalie " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalie " href="/user/show/4164661-amalie">Amalie </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4164661-amalie?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4164661-amalie?shelf=british-lit">british-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4164661-amalie?shelf=arthurian">arthurian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124082561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1375793651281959737">“If Malory's "Le Morte D'Arthur" is the skeleton of Arthurian literature then Tennyson's "Idylls of the King” is its flesh and blood”, I’ve seen this praising phrase in several places and it’s quite true. This is an epic poem containing twelve loosely connected stories/poems narrating the adventures and romances of the King Arthur and the knights of Camelot, so what’s not to love. <br/><br/>Each section deals with story a knight or several knights of King Arthur's court. Their adventures and romances are </span>
  <span id="freeText1375793651281959737" style="display:none">“If Malory's "Le Morte D'Arthur" is the skeleton of Arthurian literature then Tennyson's "Idylls of the King” is its flesh and blood”, I’ve seen this praising phrase in several places and it’s quite true. This is an epic poem containing twelve loosely connected stories/poems narrating the adventures and romances of the King Arthur and the knights of Camelot, so what’s not to love. <br /><br />Each section deals with story a knight or several knights of King Arthur's court. Their adventures and romances are only remotely connected, but there is a back story to each because something is always happening behind the scenes. The first part deals with the coming of King Arthur and of the glory of his kingdom. The second part focuses on the gradual decline of his glorious era and the King's discovery of Lancelot and Guinevere's affair (Yes, sadly the fall of great Kingdoms always seem to be women’s faults, whether it is Guinevere, Isolde or dozens of others.) <br /><br />Still this is one of my favourite pieces of Tennyson. Main features of his poetry are simplicity, nobility, and order on which his works are built and all these are visible in this great epic. There is no doubt, after reading this, that his lyrics and romantic tales as “Idylls” shall be loved and admired as long as the English language and literature last. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1375793651281959737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('b1kA+H9iuW4sXFcoq89oNY+d46LNIQvQtBZRSQSj1h8giklEWpk2PPaSzblahX5kbiki+5WmEaPcSJggJzfJVQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2SSutsTQg1J8OCtnXD2C/jMe8bBmuJN8dhXVsscBT1mW9+cK4SsMAKb2sfatd5Sv0qow6T4/iQ8eSxzb5JVQEw==')})
  }

The Knight of the Cart is the first telling of the adulterous rel<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7853891686970249308\" style=\"display:none\">Taking the legends surrounding King Arthur and weaving in new psychological elements of personal desire and courtly manner, Chrétien de Troyes fashioned a new form of medieval Romance. The Knight of the Cart is the first telling of the adulterous relationship between Lancelot and Arthur\'s Queen Guinevere, and in The Knight with the Lion Yvain neglects his bride in his quest for greater glory. Erec and Enide explores a knight\'s conflict between love and honour, Cligés exalts the possibility of pure love outside marriage, while the haunting The Story of the Grail chronicles the legendary quest. //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_455219'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/455219.The_Mabinogion?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Mabinogion<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5158478.Anonymous?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Anonymous<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.95 avg rating &mdash; 6,416 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1410\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17546670320862189893\">The Mabinogion (Welsh pronunciation: mabɪˈnɔɡjɔn) is a collection of 11 prose stories collated from medieval Welsh manuscripts. The tales draw on pre-Christian Celtic mythology, international folktale motifs and early medieval historical traditions. <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17546670320862189893\" style=\"display:none\">The Mabinogion (Welsh pronunciation: mabɪˈnɔɡjɔn) is a collection of 11 prose stories collated from medieval Welsh manuscripts. The tales draw on pre-Christian Celtic mythology, international folktale motifs and early medieval historical traditions. While some details may hark back to older Iron Age traditions, each of the tales is the product of a developed medieval Welsh narrative tradition, both oral and written.\n\nLady Charlotte Guest in the mid 19th century was the first to publish English translations of the collection, popularising the name &quot;Mabinogion&quot;. The stories appear in either or both of two medieval Welsh manuscripts, the White Book of Rhydderch or Llyfr Gwyn Rhydderch, written c.1350, and the Red Book of Hergest or Llyfr Coch Hergest, written c.1382 – 1410, tho texts or fragments of some of the tales have been preserved in earlier 13th century and later mss.\n\nScholars agree that the tales are older than the existing mss, but disagree over just how much older. The different texts originated at different times. Debate has focused on the dating of the Four Branches of the Mabinogi. Sir Ifor Williams offered a date prior to 1100, based on linguistic and historical arguments, while later Saunders Lewis set forth a number of arguments for a date between 1170 and 1190; Th Charles-Edwards, in a paper published in 1970, discussed both viewpoints, and while critical of the arguments of both scholars, noted that the language of the stories fits the 11th century. More recently, Patrick Sims-Williams argued for a plausible range of about 1060 to 1200, the current scholarly consensus.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17546670320862189893\" href=\"#\" In Freedom\'s Cause recounts the Scots\' desperate but ultimately triumphant struggle in the face of overwhelming odds - a hard-fought series of battles conducted under the leadership of William Wallace and Robert Bruce.\n\nTime has burnished the feats of these great heroes to mythic proportions, but Wallace and Bruce were real people. This gripping tale of courage, loyalty, and ingenuity recounts their deeds within an accurate historical context. Readers join their company alongside a fictional protagonist, young Archie Forbes, whose estates have been wrongfully confiscated. Archie forms a group of scouts to fight alongside the legendary Scottish chieftains (who were memorably portrayed in the film Braveheart) for their country\'s independence.\n\nIn Freedom\'s Cause is one among the many historical novels for young readers by George Alfred Henty. Storytellers.&quot; Immensely popular and widely used in schools for many years, Henty\'s novels continue to fire young imaginations with their spirited tales of adventure amid exciting historical       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13638502.Alfred_Tennyson">About Alfred Tennyson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13638502.Alfred_Tennyson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454788521p3/13638502.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/13638502.Alfred_Tennyson">Alfred Tennyson
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,179
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4848440505355331777">Alfred Tennyson, invariably known as <b>Alfred Lord Tennyson</b> on all his books, was born in Somersby, Lincolnshire, the fourth of the twelve children of George Tennyson, clergyman, and his wife, Elizabeth. In 1816 Tennyson was sent to Louth Grammar School, which he disliked so intensely that from 1820 he was educated at home until at the age of 18 he joined his two brothers at Trinity College, Cambrid</span>
  <span id="freeText4848440505355331777" style="display:none">Alfred Tennyson, invariably known as <b>Alfred Lord Tennyson</b> on all his books, was born in Somersby, Lincolnshire, the fourth of the twelve children of George Tennyson, clergyman, and his wife, Elizabeth. In 1816 Tennyson was sent to Louth Grammar School, which he disliked so intensely that from 1820 he was educated at home until at the age of 18 he joined his two brothers at Trinity College, Cambridge and with his brother Charles published his first book, <i>Poems by Two Brothers</i> the same year. <br /><br />His second book, <i>Poems Chiefly Lyrical</i> was published in 1830. In 1833, Tennyson's best friend Arthur Henry Hallam, who was engaged to his sister, died, inspiring some of his best work including <i>In Memoriam</i>, <i>Ulysses</i> and the <i>Passing of Arthur</i>.<br /><br />In 1850, following William Wordsworth, Tennyson was appointed Poet Laureate and married his childhood friend, Emily Sellwood. They had two children, Hallam born in 1852 and Lionel, two years later. In 1884, as a great favourite of both Queen Victoria and Prince Albert, he was raised to the peerage and was thereafter known as Baron Tennyson of Aldworth. He was the first Englishman to be granted such a high rank solely for literary distinction. <br /><br />Tennyson continued to write poetry throughout his life and in the 1870s also wrote a number of plays. he died in 1892 at the age of 83 and was buried in Westminster Abbey.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4848440505355331777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
